    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2197815843" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2197815843" />
    <a title="Veronica " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7035111-veronica"><img alt="Veronica " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360466651p2/7035111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2197815843?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronica " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronica " href="/user/show/7035111-veronica">Veronica </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451463890" href="/book/show/9732865-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7035111-veronica?shelf=buddy-read">buddy-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7035111-veronica?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2197815843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2617458704821315204"><b>
  <i>"Because paths can and do fork, in ways no man or woman can ever truly grasp, for that is the way the world had been made."</i>
</b><br/> <br/>Shen Tai is a young man who, for the past two years, has worked in near isolation in an effort to pay honor to his deceased father, a well-known former military commander, in a way that would’ve been meaningful to him in life. He has worked from dawn to dusk burying the bones of the dead soldiers – from both sides of the conflict – of one of his father’s last battles. H</span>
  <span id="freeText2617458704821315204" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"Because paths can and do fork, in ways no man or woman can ever truly grasp, for that is the way the world had been made."</i>
</b><br /> <br />Shen Tai is a young man who, for the past two years, has worked in near isolation in an effort to pay honor to his deceased father, a well-known former military commander, in a way that would’ve been meaningful to him in life. He has worked from dawn to dusk burying the bones of the dead soldiers – from both sides of the conflict – of one of his father’s last battles. He could never have known that such an act would alter the course of his life even as the Kitai empire, of which he is a loyal citizen, is set to be forever altered. Because when an unlooked for reward comes his way, his life path turns in directions he could never have foreseen.<br /> <br />I love the way that Kay writes books that feel like historical fiction with just a touch of fantasy. He has said that he uses the “prism of the fantastic to treat the matter of history” and no one does this quite as good as he does. His books are effortless to slip into because they feel so very much like our own world. This time around Kay invokes China’s great Tang Dynasty and the An Lushan rebellion. He does this by giving us the country of Kitai and the Imperial Court of Emperor Taizu, complete with all the behind-the-scenes machinations of courtiers and courtesans. It is a time when even the slightest misstep can give great offense, resulting in severe beatings or death. And as Tai attempts to navigate the politics that he’s been absent from for two years, we as readers are right there with him through it all.<br /> <br />Kay always manages to write smart, capable female characters and the women in this book are no exception. But whereas the women in, say, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104085.A_Song_for_Arbonne" title="A Song for Arbonne by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">A Song for Arbonne</a> were given power and respect because of the society in which they lived, the women in this book live in a culture that views them as tools or as mere objects of desire. They have no overt power but they nevertheless manage to influence a great deal, often at great risk to their own lives. My only qualm with this book is that a potential love interest for Tai is a green-eyed blonde. In a world so obviously inspired by ancient China, the choice to make Tai pine for a woman who represents a western, Caucasian standard of beauty is both puzzling and annoying. <br /> <br />That one qualm aside, Kay continues to amaze me with the subtlety of his writing. Whereas many writers are verbose in their attempts to club readers over the head in obvious efforts to force emotional reactions, Kay subscribes to the “less is more” school of writing. He sets the scene through his skillful and efficient use of words trusting readers to read between the lines, to find the meanings in what is hinted at but left unsaid. For me, this makes for purer and more honest feelings because I don’t come away feeling emotionally manipulated.<br /> <br />This is now my eighth book by Guy Gavriel Kay. A few of us over at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/192999" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Buddy Reads</a> have been slowly making our way through Kay’s wonderful bibliography. I’ve loved some of his works more than others but each and every book has touched me in one way or another and I consider them all to be worthy of multiple rereads. This book is no exception. While I started out thinking that this wouldn’t end up being one of my favorite Kay books, that last 30% or so corrected that initial impression and I look forward to reading my next book by him.<br /> <br /><b>
  <i>"You did what you could to shape your own peace, before you crossed over to the night and left the world behind, as all men did, to be forgotten or remembered, as time or love allowed."</i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="2617458704821315204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_112177446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112177446" />
    <a title="D. Pow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow"><img alt="D. Pow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339098707p2/2182799.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/112177446?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D. Pow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="D. Pow" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow">D. Pow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780451463302" href="/book/show/7113498-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2182799-d-pow?shelf=fantasy-sf-horror">fantasy-sf-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112177446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer148390318592760399">There was a time, I’d say from the early 90s until six or seven years ago, that Guy Gavriel Kay might have been my favorite writer. He was definitely my favorite fantasy writer- and to call him a fantasy writer is probably misleading, the fantasy elements in his books are often small and subtle-he is more a Historical Fiction writer than anything. He and I have grown apart, though. Mostly because my tastes have changed, I suspect, but also due to his last several books just not being up to his p</span>
  <span id="freeText148390318592760399" style="display:none">There was a time, I’d say from the early 90s until six or seven years ago, that Guy Gavriel Kay might have been my favorite writer. He was definitely my favorite fantasy writer- and to call him a fantasy writer is probably misleading, the fantasy elements in his books are often small and subtle-he is more a Historical Fiction writer than anything. He and I have grown apart, though. Mostly because my tastes have changed, I suspect, but also due to his last several books just not being up to his previous high standards. <i>Under Heaven</i> is a partial return to form, but only partial. A thrilling read, with some wonderful grace notes but also some odd choices in pacing and some set pieces that seemed more than just echoes of scenes from earlier Kay books. <br /><br />Most of Kay’s books take place in recognizable historical periods with slight twists of fantasy added, and a re-naming of places and reconfiguring of events so you are left with the flavor and atmosphere of that time and place but Kay is set free to let his imagination take over, unencumbered by strict facts. He has done takes on Provencal in the age of the Troubadors(my favorite- <i>A Song for Arbonne</i>, Medieval Spain, England under siege by Vikings, Byzantium at its apex and with <i>Under Heaven</i> he presents us with a subtly, artfully altered Tang Era China. <br /><br />Good Reader Kelly has a wonderful review of <i>Under Heaven</i>(it’s also a wonderful thread where many smart and worthy readers discuss the relative merits of Kay’s books- here is the link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89658580">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>). One thing she mentions in the review(and I’m paraphrasing like hell here, I haven’t read it in awhile) is that we are much more likely to love a work of Historical Fiction if the time and place call to us, resonate with us, and that is absolutely true. My all-time favorite Kay books are set in the places I have the most affinity for, places that have charged my imagination. <br /><br />There was a time that a Tang Era China reworked by Kay would have left me indifferent. The last few years however I have been absolutely devouring Tang Era poetry and trying to also bone up on the history to the extent my addled brain can. Two of the poets from that era, Tu Fu and Li Po have become all-time favorites of mine, I read from them nearly every week. Imagine my joy when Kay not only referenced these poets but made Li Po a major character, the ‘banished immortal’ himself striding across the stage, albeit re-imagined as sword master, ready to kick ass when he isn’t reciting poetry or chasing ladies. How cool is that? <br /><br />The main arc of the story in <i>Under Heaven</i> follows Shen Tai, whose father, a prominent general, has recently died. As a grieving rite Shen Tai has spent the last year burying the dead at the site of a major battle. The ghosts that haunt this place are depicted in chilling, uncanny fashion. Kay has always been good at capturing just how scary and unnerving the unknown(death, the gods, women(heh)) are how much mojo a good man needs to stand up straight in the face of the numinous. As a result of his bravery Shen Tai is given a gift that beggars description and is made a player and a target in a kingdom hurtling towards civil war.<br /><br />In Shen, Kay has created a subtle, likeable protagonist. The secondary characters are all great. There are wonderful set pieces of action that are as cool as anything in ‘Crouching Dragon, Hidden Tiger’ or ‘House of Flying Daggers’. There is courtly intrigue and long, literate speeches that show Kay’s constant stance as defender of culture and beauty and the fine things in this life. There is a wonderful, eerie evocation of shamanistic practices that counter this too. <br /><br />The Women are here too. Kay is better at writing women of power better than most male fantasy writers I can think of, even if in this book there power is often hidden behind the veil of courtesan or whore- you still know that they are the equal and often the better of the men in their lives. He is also a constant celebrator of the larger feminine principle, of the great SHE at the heart of the world. Over and over in Kay’s works there is the sense of huge cosmic encounters with the archetypical feminine, of the GODDESS herself, when some male interacts with the women in his life. A rite, a dangerous mystery, a crossing of the threshold into something deeper and more beautiful. And he is a romantic too. While he writes about the joining of the sexes with maturity and a discerning eye, he also tends towards a poetic lushness in the language that can sound a bit like a bodice-ripper.<br /><br />Quibbles: The ending is rushed rushed rushed. He shoehorns years of action into twenty pages. While it’s all good I would’ve preferred more time with these characters, another book even. Most of the fates of the characters seemed cool and fair including one joining I had hoped for hundreds of pages earlier. There also scenes and arrangements of characters that too strongly recall Kay’s earlier works. On the one hand it’s mostly always good, on the other you get a little concerned he’s repeating his own chord patterns. He is still a master though, even though this is not his best, and there are more than enough moments of sheer delight to make<i>Under Heaven</i> worth the read.<br /><br />Note: Note the cover on my edition. Cool terra cotta horses. Note the cover on Good Reader Kelly’s edition. Goofy looking Asian warrior’s from a bad episode of Naruto or a Chinese Soap Opera, cheesier than a block of Velveeta. Is a bad cover worth a loss of a star? I think so. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="148390318592760399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_821998961" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/821998961" />
    <a title="Em Lost In Books" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11166121-em-lost-in-books"><img alt="Em Lost In Books" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522743986p2/11166121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/821998961?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Em Lost In Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Em Lost In Books" href="/user/show/11166121-em-lost-in-books">Em Lost In Books</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780451463302" href="/book/show/7113498-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=stand-alone-read">stand-alone-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=group-books">group-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=2010-19">2010-19</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11166121-em-lost-in-books?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer821998961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9577464990534878835">I wanted to read this for a long time. <br/><br/>Story starts with Shen Tai being gifted 250 Sardian horses (a very valuable breed) by a Tagur (a rival kingdom) princess for his work of burying the dead of a great battle between the kingdoms of Kitai and Tagur. Shen Tai had been doing this in memory of his father. On his last day (after two years of burying the dead), Shen Tai faces an assassination attempt by a Kanlin Warrior (trained and most feared warriors). Somehow Shen Tai survives this attempt. Byt</span>
  <span id="freeText9577464990534878835" style="display:none">I wanted to read this for a long time. <br /><br />Story starts with Shen Tai being gifted 250 Sardian horses (a very valuable breed) by a Tagur (a rival kingdom) princess for his work of burying the dead of a great battle between the kingdoms of Kitai and Tagur. Shen Tai had been doing this in memory of his father. On his last day (after two years of burying the dead), Shen Tai faces an assassination attempt by a Kanlin Warrior (trained and most feared warriors). Somehow Shen Tai survives this attempt. Bytsan sri Nespo, a soldier of the rival army who bring him the news of 250 sardian horses gift, makes a plan that only Shen Tai can retrieve the horses from the Tagur border. Shen Tai begins his journey to his Kitai emperor to let him know about the gift. After reaching the capital Shen Tai finds himself right in the middle of a power struggle, a battle of jealousy between two most powerful man in the empire and the impending danger of a rebellion.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>“The world could bring you poison in a jewelled cup, or surprising gifts. Sometimes you didn't know which of them it was.”</i>
</b><br /><br />More than fantasy it is a historical fiction. GGK is a master of storytelling. The way he explains events is simply beautiful. He takes his time telling about the background of the events and what led to these events. His graceful writing and lyrical prose is very engrossing till the very end. <br /><br />GGK’s stories are so different from other fantasies that I’ve read. Characters full of bravery, love, deception and compassion. His characters are very simple and the words he choose to describe them are exceptional. <br /><br />Other strong point of this story is its world building. GGK definitely does a thorough research about the world he is about to create in his books. <br /><br />It is not <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104089.Tigana" title="Tigana by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Tigana</a> but still it is a very gorgeous work. </span>

<div id="review_356083873" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356083873" />
    <a title="agata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8540972-agata"><img alt="agata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548665922p2/8540972.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/356083873?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="agata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="agata" href="/user/show/8540972-agata">agata</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007342105" href="/book/show/9255472-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">die-hard GGK fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to agata by: </span>
      SciFi and Fantasy Book Club
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8540972-agata?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8540972-agata?shelf=weggegeben">weggegeben</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer356083873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14012156479507992765">In short: Kay promises a lot, but in the end he falls short to deliver.<br/><br/>A book always comes with expectations. General ones you have towards all books and specific ones for a particular one.<br/>I read "Under heaven" with the "SciFi and Fantasy Book Club". I had not read anything by Guy Gavriel Kay before and didn't know what to expect. I was just curious and decided to give the Kindle sample a try.<br/><br/>I was imediately hooked. Poetic prose, slow and deliberate development of story and character, a familia</span>
  <span id="freeText14012156479507992765" style="display:none">In short: Kay promises a lot, but in the end he falls short to deliver.<br /><br />A book always comes with expectations. General ones you have towards all books and specific ones for a particular one.<br />I read "Under heaven" with the "SciFi and Fantasy Book Club". I had not read anything by Guy Gavriel Kay before and didn't know what to expect. I was just curious and decided to give the Kindle sample a try.<br /><br />I was imediately hooked. Poetic prose, slow and deliberate development of story and character, a familiar and yet exotic setting with hints of more to come. First signs of some supernatural forces, an assassin, an intrigue, political machinations, some fascinating characters, great suspense building. All that interwoven like an emerging tapestry.<br />I wanted to see this cloth woven, wanted to see the image developed. I bought the book. The expectations now were high.<br /><br />And then something strange happened. All the strands that got me started, disappeared - one by one. All the promises made about mysterious intrigues, powerhungry politicians, and troubled characters vanished. Vanished in lame resolutions, uninspired character building and a rising sense of loss. Like a river starting from a clear mountain spring meanders through the low lands just to seep away into sand.<br /><br />The threads of silk and velvet dissolved, replaced by mundane cotton and linnen. The tapestry became a rug.<br /><br />My reading became desperate. Desperate to find more in a book that had promised so much, and went on disappointing.<br /><br />There were some bright moments even in the later parts of the book. When there was room for the author’s prose to become enthralling: descriptions of scenery, some character's inner life evolving slowly, poetry, the beauty of a woman, a death scene like in a greek tragedy, the final farewell chapters.<br />But in the end beautiful prose is not enough, if you start out promising more.<br /><br />I find it always hard to be disappointed by a book, so I'll give Kay a second chance with one of his other books (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104101.The_Lions_of_al_Rassan" title="The Lions of al-Rassan by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lions of al-Rassan</a> maybe) recommended to me.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_176146888" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176146888" />
    <a title="Tracey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/841949-tracey"><img alt="Tracey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325394892p2/841949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176146888?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracey" href="/user/show/841949-tracey">Tracey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007342013" href="/book/show/7139892-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=horses">horses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/841949-tracey?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176146888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7688143635367656138">The story begins with one Shen Tai, second son of a great general who has just, two years and a half ago (not quite), died. The mourning period is that long, two and half years, and requires complete withdrawal from society. And Tai, as part of his mourning, to honor his father, has come back to Kuala Nor, where his father won a great victory. That victory cost his people 40,000 Kitan men – and cost the enemy, the Tagurans, 60,000 men. None of these soldiers received burial, and an unburied body</span>
  <span id="freeText7688143635367656138" style="display:none">The story begins with one Shen Tai, second son of a great general who has just, two years and a half ago (not quite), died. The mourning period is that long, two and half years, and requires complete withdrawal from society. And Tai, as part of his mourning, to honor his father, has come back to Kuala Nor, where his father won a great victory. That victory cost his people 40,000 Kitan men – and cost the enemy, the Tagurans, 60,000 men. None of these soldiers received burial, and an unburied body means a ghost – and Tai very very quickly found that, indeed, there are about 100,000 ghosts crying and screaming through the night. His self-appointed task is to bury these soldiers … or, at least as many as can be buried in two and a half years by one man. It’s mad – and, in a civilization that echoes 7th century Tang Dynasty China, steeped in honour.<br /><br />As he starts another day of digging, he is pondering where his life will take him now that the mourning period is ending – whether he will go back to what he was trying to make of himself when his father died, or … something else. And then, with the unrolling of a letter, the decision is gone from his hands. In recognition of his mad, honourable actions, the White Jade Princess Cheng-wan, a bride sent from Kitai to Tagur some twenty years ago, is – with permission – giving Tai a gift.<br /><br />“It is a large gift”, says Bytsan, the Taguran soldier who brings the scroll. He is, apparently, a master of understatement.<br /><br />He is also a man who rides one of the Dragon horses, Heavenly Horses, the magnificent, fiery steeds imported from Sardia because for all its wonders and resources Kitai does not have the grazing lands to breed great horses. They are rare, and wondrous, and coveted even by those who don’t ride.<br /><br /><i>You gave a man one of these Sardian horses to reward him greatly. You gave him four or five of those glories to exalt him above his fellows, propel him towards rank – and earn him the jealousy, possibly mortal, of those who rode the smaller horses of the steppes.<br /><br />The Princess Chang-wan, a royal consort of Tagur now thorugh twenty years of peace, had just bestowed upon him, with permission, two hundred and fifty of the dragon horses.<br /><br />That was the number. Tai read it one more time.</i><br /><br />And that quickly Tai’s life is turned inside out. Starting right then, he needs to reintegrate himself into the world again, immediately, after having been almost completely isolated for his mourning period. Starting then, he needs to start thinking like a courtier, a politician, a strategist … or he will die.<br /><br />The book starts with a tight focus on Tai, and for most of the book it keeps returning to him, in a POV so tight it might almost as well be first-person. As Tai re-enters the world, occasionally the perspective splinters off – the second chapter starts in Bytsan’s head, and later we see through the eyes of several other characters. Interestingly, the female POV’s (P’s OV) are all in the present tense; often Kay uses that for scenes of deep mysticism. The closer he draws to the Ta-Ming Palace, the more glimpses of others’ thoughts we see, the wider grows the ring of personages, and the larger his deed grows: what started off as a really quite simple gesture to honor his father is magnified – he might easily have been killed by those who saw his coming there as arrogance – and now this … Poor Tai is an ordinary man, really, second son of a great man, intelligent – but not a genius in diplomacy or poetry or any of the other skills he will need. Just an ordinary man doing his damnedest to keep himself alive.<br /><br />We’re never given the motivation for the White Jade Princess to have done this to him; either she knew exactly what the gift would do to him, or she was like those politicians who burble on about healthcare reform when they’ve never had to worry about how to pay for a doctor’s visit, or cut unemployment benefits when they’ve never had to worry about how to buy groceries this weekend – - or, indeed, like Malcolm bloody Forbes pondering the mistake so many Americans make in not doing what they love for a living. It may have been a combination – “some is good, more is better” and “stir the pot”… I would have enjoyed one (present-tense) passage illuminating what was in her mind, but as it is the mystery works just fine. Kay is not one to answer all your questions – that’s part of the reality of his worlds.<br /><br />Happily for Tai, he has good friends, and finds others along the way – and he has the intelligence to know that he does not have the skills to navigate the treacherous waters of the court. So he goes his own way, and does the unexpected – and it’s a joy to watch. I loved this character. He’s not my favorite – <i>Tigana</i> has most of those – but Tai is a wonderful companion. He has flaws, he’s aware of his flaws, and he does not react to much of anything in just the way the reader or the people around him expect. Can’t ask for much more than that. He reacted to political intrigue and webs being spun around him and plots surfacing and submerging again (and how’s that for a mixed metaphorical bag) much the way I think I, or any reasonably intelligent but unversed person, would react if thrown into the middle of that mess – step by step, and thinking fast, terrified and exhilarated and praying a lot…<br /><br />I loved Tai. I loved Spring Rain and wanted her to be triumphant in the end. I loved Wei Song, and how she skirted (heh) the stereotypes. I loved Sima Zian even more. I hated Tai’s brother – and then not so much – and only Kay can leave you hating a character but utterly respecting him at the same time. I think the only thing I could have wished for would have been … more of the horses.<br /><br />Only Kay can take an action in a character’s past (here, what happened with Meshag – the ending of which I have to say I thought was a horrible mistake) and spin its repercussions through the action of the current story like this. Only Kay can withhold the information, and withhold it a little more, and then take you right out of the current story into the past in such a way that not only is it not a jarring interruption to the current narrative, it’s the satisfaction of a need to know, and so far beyond info-dump that everyone who aspires to write should study it.<br /><br />Only Kay can take a world as alien to modern America as Tang Dynasty China, and make it so comprehensible and fascinating and, still, so mysterious and complex …<br /><br />Only Kay (and a very few others) can flood a narrative with art and light like this.<br /><br />Only Kay (and a very few others) – can set up a situation which is so intensely painful, and so very inevitable, but still so unpredictable as the scene in the inn yard. Only Kay can show so clearly, so vividly, so, sometimes, painfully how the course of a life, a love, a kingdom can turn on the decision of a moment, on a word spoken (or not) or heeded (or not). Only Kay can spin out from an intense focus on a single character to a global view and back again with such skill and clarity. Only Kay can write something so simultaneously gritty and lyric, so painful and euphoric. Only Kay writes like this – which is terrible, because such extraordinariness takes time … but which is good, because if every book was this intense reading would be an exhausting process.<br /><br /><i>Under Heaven</i> was somehow not as wrenching as others – I’ve said before how devastating <i>Tigana</i> was the first time I read it; it’s in anticipation of something like that that I will not read Kay anywhere but at home in private. It goes toward what I saw on SYTYCD, with the dancers shaken and somber after emotional performances, and how I’ve felt coming out of some films (<i>Schindler’s List</i>, for a prime example): there are some works of art that leave one unready to return to normal life and ordinary company for a while. They need space before, to prepare, if possible, and most certainly space after. (I’ve often thought that there should be some kind of airlock in theatres where someone could go to recover a bit before going out into the world – especially into daylight, which just seems wrong sometimes.) I didn’t cry at the inn yard, which surprised me a little even as I was reading. I did cry at the end, which was as inevitable as the events of the inn yard. It’s not my favorite of Kay’s books – I really do need to read <i>Tigana</i> again – and <i>Arbonne</i> - and <i>Al-Rassan</i> – and the Sarantine Mosaic… but Kay’s writing on his worst day is so far superior to anyone else’s that “favorite” is almost irrelevant. It’s a joy and an honor just to open his books. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7688143635367656138" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2848243797" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2848243797" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2848243797?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2848243797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11950458338829286834"><i>Under Heaven</i> is a fantasy-ish story set in medieval China. There are fantastical elements, and they do play a pivotal role, but they aren’t at the forefront. My knowledge of Chinese history and geography is nearly non-existent, but I believe this book is based on fictitious locations and characters while being inspired by real ones. I enjoyed most aspects of the story, but I did feel like it occasionally dawdled and sometimes I got restless with it.<br/><br/>The story starts off with Shen Tai, a man who’s</span>
  <span id="freeText11950458338829286834" style="display:none"><i>Under Heaven</i> is a fantasy-ish story set in medieval China. There are fantastical elements, and they do play a pivotal role, but they aren’t at the forefront. My knowledge of Chinese history and geography is nearly non-existent, but I believe this book is based on fictitious locations and characters while being inspired by real ones. I enjoyed most aspects of the story, but I did feel like it occasionally dawdled and sometimes I got restless with it.<br /><br />The story starts off with Shen Tai, a man who’s honoring his dead father during his mourning period by burying the dead at a battle site his father had been a part of. There are ghosts there that are slowly laid to rest as he buries the corresponding bodies. He buries bodies from both sides of the battle, and his actions are at first considered strange and then admirable. The admiration has unexpected results that bring about major changes for Shen Tai. This doesn’t even remotely encompass what this story is about, but the dawdling aspect means that I can’t really describe the plot without giving away things that are revealed pretty far into the book. The story starts out as one type of story, then slowly morphs into a different type of story around the halfway mark. Meanwhile, there are also some chapters that tell Tai’s sister’s story, and that is yet another type of story.<br /><br />I’ve read a few books set in China, but not very many of them, and I enjoyed reading about a different and less-familiar setting. I don’t know how accurate the author’s portrayal of Chinese culture from that era was, but it felt believable while also being quite a bit different from the cultures portrayed in most fantasy I’ve read. I was intimidated by the names at first. The beginning of the book lists a large cast of characters with names I was afraid I would never be able to keep straight. In actuality, I didn’t need that list. The characters were introduced slowly, and the spellings among the most frequently seen characters were different enough that I was never confused. For all the characters, the author always gave the reader enough information to remember who they were when they showed back up in the story.<br /><br />I was a little distracted by the mixed tenses. Some POVs were written in present tense and others were written in past tense. I think it was mostly (only?) the female POVs written in present tense? I tended to forget about it until the present tense showed up and jarred me a bit. It did give those sections a different feel that kind of added to the atmosphere, but I’m not sure if that was the whole point or if I missed something.<br /><br />As I said, the story did occasionally feel slow when it dawdled a bit over the scene-setting and history and such, but it was never boring. I enjoyed the story and I liked the main characters. I think I was also more satisfied with the ending than I usually am with his books, although I would have enjoyed knowing more about what happened with some of the characters. One of the things I enjoy most about Guy Gavriel Kay’s writing is how he writes characters and their interactions with each other. I plan to continue on with <i>River of Stars</i> which I understand takes place in the same setting but about 400 years later.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11950458338829286834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_935143320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/935143320" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7608899-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509216859p2/7608899.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/935143320?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7608899-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007342013" href="/book/show/7139892-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer935143320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3289606407036240226">This was everything I would expect from a Guy Gavriel Kay novel - an interesting period of history that I was largely unfamiliar with (Tang Dynasty China, specifically), a bunch of profoundly human characters in all their flawed glory, and enough emotion to more than justify the kickbacks I'm sure GGK gets from Kleenex.<br/><br/>This book had a great deal of similarities to the <i>Sarantine Mosaic</i>, which is not a bad thing. History is an incredibly rich field of source material, and I dearly wish more author</span>
  <span id="freeText3289606407036240226" style="display:none">This was everything I would expect from a Guy Gavriel Kay novel - an interesting period of history that I was largely unfamiliar with (Tang Dynasty China, specifically), a bunch of profoundly human characters in all their flawed glory, and enough emotion to more than justify the kickbacks I'm sure GGK gets from Kleenex.<br /><br />This book had a great deal of similarities to the <i>Sarantine Mosaic</i>, which is not a bad thing. History is an incredibly rich field of source material, and I dearly wish more authors would venture beyond the popular understandings of medieval northern Europe.<br /><br />The only real flaw I can point to in this book is that it wasn't as good as <i>The Lions of al-Rassan</i>. As far as critiques go, that is a pretty good one. And "not GGK's best" is still solidly in the 80th percentile, so it's an easy 5 stars.</span>

That is to say, he researches the hell out of a particular time and place, and then writes in a fictionalized version of that setting, frequently with some magical elements, and almost always with two moons.<br/><br/>Note: The rest of this review has been withdrawn due to the changes in Goodreads policy and enforcement. <div id="review_440208895" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/440208895" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/440208895?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780451463302" href="/book/show/7113498-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=canadian-eh">canadian-eh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=when-words-collide">when-words-collide</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=historical-fantasy">historical-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440208895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5815595313021439596">"Shen Tai, son of an illustrious general serving the Emperor of Kitai, has spent two years honoring the memory of his late father by burying the bones of the dead from both armies at the site of one of his father's last great battles. In recognition of his labors and his filial piety, an unlikely source has sent him a dangerous gift: 250 Sardian horses.<br/><br/> You give a man one of the famed Sardian horses to reward him greatly. You give him four or five to exalt him above his fellows, propel him towar</span>
  <span id="freeText5815595313021439596" style="display:none">"Shen Tai, son of an illustrious general serving the Emperor of Kitai, has spent two years honoring the memory of his late father by burying the bones of the dead from both armies at the site of one of his father's last great battles. In recognition of his labors and his filial piety, an unlikely source has sent him a dangerous gift: 250 Sardian horses.<br /><br /> You give a man one of the famed Sardian horses to reward him greatly. You give him four or five to exalt him above his fellows, propel him towards rank, and earn him jealousy, possibly mortal jealousy. Two hundred and fifty is an unthinkable gift, a gift to overwhelm an emperor."<br /><br />Exquisitely written, completely engrossing and extremely satisfying. Shen Tai is an unusual man--thoughtful, but also a man of action. A scholar, but capable with a weapon. He must decide if he will pursue the dangerous life of a man at court when he returns from his self-imposed exile among the ghosts of the battlefield, and he must make his decision at full speed with assassins pursuing him and members of the Imperial Court trying to use him.<br /><br />Kay's poetic writing is perfect for this Tang Dynasty China adventure. There is rarely a word out of place. I will look forward to reading the next book in this setting, River of Stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5815595313021439596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_851921875" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/851921875" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/851921875?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780451463302" href="/book/show/7113498-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=far-east">far-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer851921875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11648818916489362508">So how much trouble could 250 horses be? I mean, besides feeding them and keeping them in shape it can't be that bad, right? <br/><br/>Well, if these horses happen to be highly prized by very powerful people (including an Emperor) AND you are stuck in the middle of nowhere when you receive the gift you can find yourself in a bit of a pickle. This is the situation Shen Tai finds himself in when he is gifted (though gifted might not be how he sees it) 250 magnificent Sardian horses, horses whose qualities f</span>
  <span id="freeText11648818916489362508" style="display:none">So how much trouble could 250 horses be? I mean, besides feeding them and keeping them in shape it can't be that bad, right? <br /><br />Well, if these horses happen to be highly prized by very powerful people (including an Emperor) AND you are stuck in the middle of nowhere when you receive the gift you can find yourself in a bit of a pickle. This is the situation Shen Tai finds himself in when he is gifted (though gifted might not be how he sees it) 250 magnificent Sardian horses, horses whose qualities far surpass all others available to the great Kitai Empire (Kay's name for what we call the Tang Dynasty of China), of which Shen Tai is a citizen of.<br /><br />Thrust into this precarious situation Shen Tai strikes out to discharge this gift before it gets him killed. Having been away from the Kitai Empire for two years he is unprepared for the volatile political environment his appearance and gift unsettles. There are may layers of court intrigue, hidden agendas, and good old fashion personal grudges.<br /><br />I think the best way to describe Under Heaven was achingly beautiful. The characters in this book were vibrant and nuanced, the setting was beautifully crafted and multi-layered, the story was both grand and personal, and the writing was elegant and well balanced, never saying more or less than was needed. There is a refined beauty in the economy of language and the imagery Kay employed to tell this story. It sweeps you up and places you firmly among the characters and events of the book.<br /><br />Kay does a marvelous job balancing three story telling devices: the perspective of the main characters, the perspective of the tangential characters who we only see briefly but fall into the orbit of the main characters, and the greater picture of the events going on beyond the characters' awareness. Each passage, even those from tangential characters we will never see or hear from again, enriches and deepens the setting and atmosphere. Some of my favorite sections were from these characters' perspective, letting us see the main characters from a more impartial position, providing another view on the events going on, and just being delightful to read on their own merits.<br /><br />I was initially put off by some of the third person passages that cropped up near the end of the book that provide a sweeping view of some events occurring outside the main character's view but it all came together at the end to provide what I think was the message of the book: decisions matter. Large, small, well planned, spur of the moment, all of them in some way contribute to the human experience. A simple, off the cuff decision could have extensive repercussions in a year's time. Empires could fall, famine could spread, love could be unrequited, the path not taken could have been ruin or paradise. <br /><br />All we can do is make the best decisions we have available to us and move on with our lives knowing that some decisions we can control and other decisions control us. Be it the strict imperial protocol of a fragile empire or what inn we choose to stop at. Life and love may not turn out as we expect or play out like a fairy tale but we must make the best of it and continue to live life as we best see fit. To quote the ever entertaining movie Gladiator: What we do in life echos an eternity.<br /><br /><i>Under Heaven</i>'s story about intersecting lives, decisions, and consequences poignantly conveys this message with subtlety and beauty rarely found in literature.<br /><br />(For those of you who want to read about the actual historical circumstances that inspired this book, check out the Wikipedia article on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" rel="nofollow noopener">An Lushan Rebellion</a>)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11648818916489362508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Mogsy (MMOGC)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4379514-mogsy-mmogc"><img alt="Mogsy (MMOGC)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286325553p2/4379514.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mogsy (MMOGC)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mogsy (MMOGC)" href="/user/show/4379514-mogsy-mmogc">Mogsy (MMOGC)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670068098" href="/book/show/7113496-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124918415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10622386466834228206">A historical fantasy set in far-away Kitai, a land inspired by Tang Dynasty China. One of my favorite books of all time is The Lions of Al-Rassan by Kay, so this fact along with my interest in imperial Chinese history made this book a must-read. Under Heaven tells the story of a middle son of a Kitan general who spends two years in the mountains burying the bones of soldiers from a war that took place there, and is given 250 “heavenly” Sardian horses for his honorable deed. This extravagant gift</span>
  <span id="freeText10622386466834228206" style="display:none">A historical fantasy set in far-away Kitai, a land inspired by Tang Dynasty China. One of my favorite books of all time is The Lions of Al-Rassan by Kay, so this fact along with my interest in imperial Chinese history made this book a must-read. Under Heaven tells the story of a middle son of a Kitan general who spends two years in the mountains burying the bones of soldiers from a war that took place there, and is given 250 “heavenly” Sardian horses for his honorable deed. This extravagant gift immediately thrusts him into a world of palace intrigue and political drama, and the result is a beautifully written “history-based fantasy” that’s sometimes tragic and sometimes suspenseful, but filled with memorable characters and intricate plots throughout. As always, I appreciate the immense detail Kay puts into his books, which makes the world of Kitai come to life. The characters are believable — not perfect but definitely “human”. Anyway, I don’t want to spoil too much of it, just know I highly recommend this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10622386466834228206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2524368036" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2524368036" />
    <a title="Gabi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84001290-gabi"><img alt="Gabi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593952673p2/84001290.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2524368036?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabi" href="/user/show/84001290-gabi">Gabi</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer11871713364343774606">3.5 stars - rounded down.<br/>"The Lions of Al-Rassan" I did round up because I was so invested in the characters and loved the beginning. Here it is the other way round. The slow pace combined with a - for me - not very interesting main character left me with only admiring the prose and the atmosphere Kay is creating. This alone is worth my time, no question, but for a story that long I'd rather have an engaging narration as a plus. The female side characters all were much more interesting than the </span>
  <span id="freeText11871713364343774606" style="display:none">3.5 stars - rounded down.<br />"The Lions of Al-Rassan" I did round up because I was so invested in the characters and loved the beginning. Here it is the other way round. The slow pace combined with a - for me - not very interesting main character left me with only admiring the prose and the atmosphere Kay is creating. This alone is worth my time, no question, but for a story that long I'd rather have an engaging narration as a plus. The female side characters all were much more interesting than the MC and I would have wished for more 'screentime' for each of them.<br /><br />The last quarter then managed to get me emotionally involved like I wished I could have been from the start. There were some outstandingly beautifully written scenes. Yet when balanced against the whole of the book I can not give a fourth star for that. <br />Perhaps it would have worked better if some of the middle parts had been tightened.<br /><br />I still was glad to have listened to it, because Kay's prose always enchants me. But it pales in comparison to "The Lions of Al-Rassan" or especially "Tigana".</span>

<div id="review_180026428" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180026428" />
    <a title="Alice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1563863-alice"><img alt="Alice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222351664p2/1563863.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180026428?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice" href="/user/show/1563863-alice">Alice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007342013" href="/book/show/7139892-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180026428" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3844311907960667218">I'll preface my review by saying that I'm a long-time fan of Kay; he's a fixture on my list of favourite authors. <br/><br/>That being said - <br/><br/>I found <i>Under Heaven</i> quite disappointing. From the [lack of] plot to the [extremely under-developed] characters, the whole novel felt very wanting. <br/><br/>Based on Kay's better works (e.g. <i>Tigana, A Song for Arbonne</i>, etc. ) we know that he could have easily made this into a real classic, with characters and lines and imagery that stay with you years after you've read them.</span>
  <span id="freeText3844311907960667218" style="display:none">I'll preface my review by saying that I'm a long-time fan of Kay; he's a fixture on my list of favourite authors. <br /><br />That being said - <br /><br />I found <i>Under Heaven</i> quite disappointing. From the [lack of] plot to the [extremely under-developed] characters, the whole novel felt very wanting. <br /><br />Based on Kay's better works (e.g. <i>Tigana, A Song for Arbonne</i>, etc. ) we know that he could have easily made this into a real classic, with characters and lines and imagery that stay with you years after you've read them. <br /><br />In <i>Under Heaven</i>, you're given a very underwhelming plot - the premise (the gift of the 250 Sardian horses) is promising, but one that never delivers. We never find out <i>why</i> the White Jade Princess decided on this outrageous gift! I kept waiting to come to the part where we're taken to that palace where she lives, to her mind, to her thoughts about it etc. Kay's great in his works about hopping around from locale to locale, from one end of the continent to the other - he took us to Sardia, he took us beyond the Wall, why did he leave out one crucial, integral set of characters? This baffles me. <br /><br />Also - the reason for an assassin being sent to kill him ... I can't be alone, surely, in thinking that the reason was weak, lame, and a total anti-climactic cop-out? When they revealed the reason, I was literally like, "Really? <i>Really?</i> No... just, no."<br /><br />The fact that the horses actually play such a small part in the story itself, also bothered me a little. <br /><br />Shen Tai, his sister, his brothers, his lovers - all had interesting bases, all were fairly two dimensional. I would have loved to have gotten to know them all better, if he'd let us. I kept suspecting that this book was written by someone else, only based on Kay's ideas. <br /><br />Having read his better works, honestly this felt like a half-hearted attempt at a book, to satisfy a contract or to get his publishers off his back or something. :(</span>
  <a data-text-id="3844311907960667218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11971381707075174483">8.5/10<br/><br/>In <i>Under Heaven</i>, Kay takes us to the 8th century China, evoking the Tang Dynasty and the An Lushan rebellion, in a world very different from the ones he has showed in his previous books; and into a story full with wonderful characters, beautiful poetry and lots of intrigues and some even greater action scenes.<br/><br/>The story follows Tai, the second son of the great General Shen Gao, who for two years now mourns the loss of his father (as it is required for their homage) and lives in a place wher</span>
  <span id="freeText11971381707075174483" style="display:none">8.5/10<br /><br />In <i>Under Heaven</i>, Kay takes us to the 8th century China, evoking the Tang Dynasty and the An Lushan rebellion, in a world very different from the ones he has showed in his previous books; and into a story full with wonderful characters, beautiful poetry and lots of intrigues and some even greater action scenes.<br /><br />The story follows Tai, the second son of the great General Shen Gao, who for two years now mourns the loss of his father (as it is required for their homage) and lives in a place where great wars have taken place between the Kitai and their enemies, the Tagurans, buring the dead as he tries to give to their spirits their final rest; before the night comes again and they start screaming, wailing and scaring anyone who comes near.<br />But when on day the White Jade Princess, the wife of the Taguran Emperor, gives him a gift, a great gift that hasn’t be given to anyone before, to honor him for the work he has done those two years beside the dead he will find himself against enemies that will try to kill him and take it from him; and between a game of intrigues and machinations that could bring the destruction of the Kitan Emperor and also for the whole Empire.<br /><br />The book, I must say, reminded me a lot of times scenes and places, moments and characters, that Kay has used many times before in his books such as: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49173-the-sarantine-mosaic" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Sarantine Mosaic</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104085.A_Song_for_Arbonne" title="A Song for Arbonne by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">A Song for Arbonne</a> and, perhaps, a litlte from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104089.Tigana" title="Tigana by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Tigana</a> too. <br />Although it may feel a bit too familiar - especially for those have read him before - it has its own unique moments as Kay manages to create yet again characters that cannot but amaze you and, combining with his great writting style, he bring us to another wonderful story.<br /><br />All in all, it is a book that fans of Guy Gavriel Kay's would not want to miss, as it brings all those things that loved and manages to take somewher far away, in a world that has much more to show us.<br /><br /><br />Ελληνική κριτική:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Στο <i>Under Heaven</i>, ο Kay μας ταξιδεύει στον 8ο αιώνα της Κίνας, επηρεασμένος από την δυναστεία Tang και την επανάσταση του An Lushan, σε έναν κόσμο πολύ διαφορετικό από αυτούς που έχει δείξει στα προηγούμενα βιβλία του αλλά και μια ιστορία γεμάτη με υπέροχους χαρακτήρες, με όμορφη ποίηση αλλά και αρκετή δράση.<br /><br />Η ιστορία ακολουθεί τον Tai, τον δεύτερο γιο του μεγάλου Στρατηγού Shen Gao, όπου εδώ και δύο χρόνια θρηνεί τον χαμό του πατέρα του (όπως επιβάλλει ο φόρος τιμής τους) και ζει σε ένα μέρος όπου μεγάλοι πόλεμοι είχαν λάβει χώρα μεταξύ των Kitai και των εχθρών τους, τους Tagurans, θάβοντας τους νεκρούς και προσπαθώντας έτσι να τους δώσει μια τελευταία ανάπαυση στα πνεύματα τους, πριν έρθει η νύχτα πάλι και αρχίσουν να φωνάζουν, να ουρλιάζουν θρηνώντας τρομάζοντας όποιον βρεθεί κοντά τους.<br />Όταν όμως η πριγκίπισσα και σύζυγος του αυτοκράτορα των Tagurans του στείλει ένα δώρο, ένα πολύ μεγάλο δώρο που δεν έχει δοθεί ποτέ σε κανέναν, σαν φόρο τιμής για αυτά τα δύο χρόνια που στάθηκε δίπλα στους νεκρούς θα βρεθεί αντιμέτωπος με πολλούς εχθρούς που θα θελήσουν να τον σκοτώσουν και να του το πάρουν, αλλά θα βρεθεί επίσης και ανάμεσα σε ένα παιχνίδι δολοπλοκιών που μπορεί να φέρει την καταστροφή του ίδιου αυτοκράτορα του Kitai αλλά και ολόκληρης της αυτοκρατορίας.<br /><br />Το βιβλίο αυτό πρέπει να πω σε πολλά σημεία μου θύμισε πράγματα και σκηνές που έχει χρησιμοποιήσει ο Kay ξανά πολλές φορές στα βιβλία του, και ειδικά πιο πολύ με το Σαραντίνο Ψηφιδωτό, την Αρμπόν και ακόμα, ίσως, και λίγο με το Τιγκάνα, αλλά, για άλλη μια φορά, έχει γράψει ένα πολύ καλό βιβλίο με δυνατή ιστορία και χαρακτήρες, και με την γραφή του να κυλάει υπέροχα.<br /><br />Σίγουρα ένα βιβλίο που δεν πρέπει να χάσουν οι φίλοι του Kay αλλά και όσοι αρχίζουν να γνωρίζουν τα έργα του πιστεύω είναι μια καλή αρχή. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_443289096" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/443289096" />
    <a title="Lee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4162390-lee"><img alt="Lee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525386186p2/4162390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/443289096?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee" href="/user/show/4162390-lee">Lee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007342013" href="/book/show/7139892-under-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer443289096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11623487012920060302"><b>Story: 4/5</b><br/><blockquote>
  <i>1: Being Vague, rambling plot with no little believable storyline<br/>5: Ripping yarn, clever, thought provoking</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>As a Gavriel Kay fan, I was excited to be reading some of his work again, as it has been a while. This story is different from most of this work, (maybe somewhat in the vein of Lions of al-Rassan) with a very strong storyline based around political intrigue of an empire reminiscent of a japanese or chinese emperor. There are very few action scenes in this story with the main p</span>
  <span id="freeText11623487012920060302" style="display:none"><b>Story: 4/5</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>1: Being Vague, rambling plot with no little believable storyline<br />5: Ripping yarn, clever, thought provoking</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />As a Gavriel Kay fan, I was excited to be reading some of his work again, as it has been a while. This story is different from most of this work, (maybe somewhat in the vein of Lions of al-Rassan) with a very strong storyline based around political intrigue of an empire reminiscent of a japanese or chinese emperor. There are very few action scenes in this story with the main plot being based around a returning soldier with a gift of amazing value (lots of prized horses). This gift makes our soldier the most wanted man in the kingdom, as different factions position themselves to be part of the gift. This gift means a death sentence unless the main man can find a way to make himself as important to the empire as the horses themselves. I am not a big fan of political intrigue, backstabbing and posturing in the court, but have to admit, there was a lot to like here. What made this whole story interesting was the honour system in this type of culture, where bowing not deep enough or long enough, can have you executed. Where a slight to a family can result in your required suicide. <br />The political banter of the protagonist and his opponents is brilliantly written and you can feel the frustration of the characters as decorum and honour dictates their speech and next moves. This verbal sword play was every bit as exciting as the actual moments of physical action.<br />There is very little magic or fantasy in this story, there is a brief moment where ghosts join in the story in the first few chapters, a guy who speaks with wolves, but after that there is no reference to anything out of the ordinary. Very similar to GGK’s Lions of al-Rassan’s lack of magic. <br /><br /><b>Characters: 3 /5</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>1: Unrealistic/unbelievable. Feel nothing for these characters<br />5: Fully engaged with the characters, believable. Researched.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I am on the fence with the characters. Usually GGK builds very strong emotional characters, but with this book I really struggled to engage with them. It could be because of the reserved persona that each character has due to the setting, I don’t know. But already each character is beginning to fade from memory and I can hardly recall their names. So I am rating the characters lower than I would have expected and lower than the books finally score. Which for me is unusual as generally I need to believe and have feelings for the characters to rate the story four stars. <br /> <br /><b>Read Weight: Heavy </b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Fluffy, Light, Solid, Heavy, Struggle</i>
</blockquote><br />The story is very simple, yet because of the dialogue required to pull off the intrigue and depth of the culture, it does make the book a heavy read. <br /><br /><b>Engagement: 4 /5</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>1: Not fussed about finishing<br />5: Could stay up all night</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I found that as the story developed I found it hard to put down. An indication of that was on a recent flight. I took the book to read when the instructions to turn off my kindle for take off meant I had 15 minutes down time for reading. This is where I usually read a ‘real’ book, until I can get the kindle back out. In the last third of the book, i stuck with this story for the flight.<br /><br /><br /><b>Recommend: 4/5</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>1: Would advise you to read something else<br />5: Go read it now. It is THAT good</i>
</blockquote><br /><br /><br />Overall i enjoyed the story very much, I was pleasantly surprised as the initial story had me wondering if this would be something I could get into. As an author I like Gavriel Kays work and he hasn’t let me down with this book.<br />If you enjoyed the Feist, Wurts Empire trilogy I can see you enjoying this very much. For those who enjoy the asian emperor/dynasty stories this would also suit your needs. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11623487012920060302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1086942025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3145391787494340749">This was a really wonderful book. I was told that it had a very slow and steady pace and I found that this worked really well for me. I'm not familiar with the dynasties of China and the character names were a bit more difficult for me. Additionally, I was using the audio. So the way that the characters were introduced one by one, leaving time to get to know them before another was introduced, really worked. The pace did pick up, as did the complexity. I was with it right up to the moment that p</span>
  <span id="freeText3145391787494340749" style="display:none">This was a really wonderful book. I was told that it had a very slow and steady pace and I found that this worked really well for me. I'm not familiar with the dynasties of China and the character names were a bit more difficult for me. Additionally, I was using the audio. So the way that the characters were introduced one by one, leaving time to get to know them before another was introduced, really worked. The pace did pick up, as did the complexity. I was with it right up to the moment that politics became important. Politics make my brain short out :) So yes, I got confused in there, but I really loved the story. I loved that the wrap-up wasn't entirely what I expected but was still appropriate and still happy.<br /><br />To all my buddies who haven't read this - get on it already!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3145391787494340749" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
